<div id="review_2829347301" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2829347301" />
    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39346323-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513962691p2/39346323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2829347301?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/39346323-greg">Greg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=mid-20th-century-american-crime">mid-20th-century-american-crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=hard-boiled-private-eye">hard-boiled-private-eye</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2829347301" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3328755046566998141">Book 226 - Mid-20th Century North American Crime Readathon - Round 10<br/>Chandler isn't famous for plot and resolution. But here, the plot is linear, makes sense, has great clues and red herrings, has a good series of investigations, and has an ambiguous ending: you just can't keep the girl in Kansas after she's been chasing her own Oscar in Hollywood. I've now read all 7 of Chandler's novels, PLUS I've read "The Annotated Big Sleep" and that book added so much to my understanding of Chandler, a ver</span>
  <span id="freeText3328755046566998141" style="display:none">Book 226 - Mid-20th Century North American Crime Readathon - Round 10<br />Chandler isn't famous for plot and resolution. But here, the plot is linear, makes sense, has great clues and red herrings, has a good series of investigations, and has an ambiguous ending: you just can't keep the girl in Kansas after she's been chasing her own Oscar in Hollywood. I've now read all 7 of Chandler's novels, PLUS I've read "The Annotated Big Sleep" and that book added so much to my understanding of Chandler, a very flawed, very odd, often flat-out exhausted, close to dementia at times character. Chandler writes in "Annotated Big Sleep' he wanted to 'burlesque' his novels: I'd say that yes, he did indeed. He imitated Hammett extensively (nothing wrong with imitating one of the greats) and his intention was often a parody of the entire genre. To me, that's exactly what he did with 5 of his novels, but not this one or "Lady in the Lake."<br />HOOK - 4 stars &gt;&gt;&gt;The pebbled glass door panel is lettered in flaked black paint "Pillip Marlowe...Investigations. It is a reasonably shabby door at the end of a reasonably shabby corridor...&lt;&lt;&lt; opens this novel. It's a straight-up description of Marlowe's office and it reads like a SCENE line from a film script. But this novel IS about Hollywood, and after finishing this very good Hollywood story, the script-like opening simply works perfectly. After all, this IS to be a script, and is filmed.<br />PACE - 4-The plot flows, this is a nice 2-sit read/2-day read. <br />PLOT -5- I'll quote from the back book blurb: "A movie starlet with a gangster boyfriend and a pair of siblings with a shared secret lure Marlowe into the less than glamorous and more than a little dangerous world of Hollywood fame. There is line after line in this book about Hollywood, I loved this exchange:<br />***<br />Marlowe: "So I was wondering. What name does your sister Leila use in pictures?"<br />Orfamy (yep, that's the young ladies name): "Pictures? Oh you mean motion pictures. Why I never said she was in pictures. I never said anything like that." (Absolutely not true)<br />Marlowe gives Orfamy a 'big homely lopsided grin.<br />Orfamy: "You leave my sister Leila out of your dirty remarks."<br />Marlowe: "what dirty remarks? Or should I try go guess?<br />Orfamy (screaming): "All you think about is liquor and women. I hate you!"<br />Then the next morning Marlowe is parked a couple of doors from the Bay City Camera Shop.<br />***<br />Open this book anywhere and you'll find lines like "Malibu. More movie stars. More pinkd and blue bathtubs. More tufted beds. More Chanel No. 5...More wind-blown hair and sunglasses and attitudes..." This, to me, is one of the great novels about Hollywood. And Hollywood CAN be kind, yes,to the truly talented....that'd be about .001% of the people in America tilted Southwest to the land of dreams and early deaths.<br />CAST: 4: Marlowe is a mess: "Hold it, Marlowe, you're not human tonight." Then, "Here we go again. You're not human tonight." Then on the next page Marlowe ruminates: "Maybe I'm an ectoplasm with a private license. Maybe we all get like this in the cold half-lit world where always the wrong thing happens and never the right." Offamy Quest as a quiet little church-mouse from Kansas puts on quite a performance, telling Marlowe she is looking for her brother, Orrin P Quest. Even the names are send-ups of an old Hollywood custom of changing names, and what's this with OPQ of Orrin P Quest. (Red herring?) I kept trying to descrable Offamy's name and...well...another red herring? Lester B Clausen (as in 'be' a 'Clausen pickle) is murdered with an ice pick, which is a perfect way to pull a pickle from a pickle jar. More fun and games from Chandler. Then, there is BIG STAR Mavis Weld, but who names a big star Mavis? (No one if they want to draw money.) And of course there is a Miss Gonzalez...it's Southern California and Chandler never shies away from a little culture mash-up. VERY good cast! Oh, and I almost forgot Beifus who supplies us with a very typical Chandler line directed toward Marlowe: "Maybe I'm a queer, but for me you don't have no more sex appeal than a turtle." But you just know in this burlesque paradoy, Beifus has lots of turtles at home.<br />ATMOSPHERE - 5-Brilliant. Everything Chandler has to say about Hollywood rings absolutely true. The studio systems then controlled everyone, and if a studio has a BIG A STAR pulling in the bucks, the even more talented GREAT STAR on the sidelines doesn't have a chance: she's tied into the system and can only make movies for that one studio, which already has their A STAR. By the time this novel was published, Chandler was writing screenplays. He had the inside scoop, the dirt. And he lays it on the line in this book.<br />SUMMARY: 4.4 stars. Again, this is my favorite Chandler novel. He knows Hollywood, lives Hollywood, and delivers a complicated plot that comes to a perfect conclusion. If you only read one Chandler book, I reccomend this one: it's close to a crime masterpiece.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3328755046566998141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11266924180329983171">This is Chandler at his cheekiest, with wisecracking Marlowe in top form as he goes toe to toe with with mobsters, the LAPD and Hollywood. Marlowe also appears more introspective, self critical and vulnerable than I've seen him, giving him some more emotional depth and soul. The missing persons turned murder case plot arc initially feels a bit formulaic for Chandler, but becomes muddled as the story develops and a bunch of tenuous plot threads and supporting characters are introduced that are di</span>
  <span id="freeText11266924180329983171" style="display:none">This is Chandler at his cheekiest, with wisecracking Marlowe in top form as he goes toe to toe with with mobsters, the LAPD and Hollywood. Marlowe also appears more introspective, self critical and vulnerable than I've seen him, giving him some more emotional depth and soul. The missing persons turned murder case plot arc initially feels a bit formulaic for Chandler, but becomes muddled as the story develops and a bunch of tenuous plot threads and supporting characters are introduced that are difficult to make sense of after a point. The climax comes early, with the story meandering in the final chapters as Marlowe chews events over and fits the pieces together. Not Chandler's greatest work, but still wonderful.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11023479541406075974"><b>Mini Review</b><br/><br/>The most cynical book in Chandler's oeuvre, the quick wit of the initial books in the series had been modified into an unflinching bitterness. It's almost as though the gloves are off, Chandler is weary off all the saccharine hypocrisy of the world and he calls it out as he sees it and it makes for spectacular reading.<br/><br/>The corrupt city with its cynical cops, decadent dames with their starlet sisters and off course Marlowe himself round off an impressive cast. The plot is turned and twi</span>
  <span id="freeText11023479541406075974" style="display:none"><b>Mini Review</b><br /><br />The most cynical book in Chandler's oeuvre, the quick wit of the initial books in the series had been modified into an unflinching bitterness. It's almost as though the gloves are off, Chandler is weary off all the saccharine hypocrisy of the world and he calls it out as he sees it and it makes for spectacular reading.<br /><br />The corrupt city with its cynical cops, decadent dames with their starlet sisters and off course Marlowe himself round off an impressive cast. The plot is turned and twisted so many times on itself, following it to its shocking conclusion is a rewarding struggle in itself. But for me more than the characters and plot, a Chandler book is an excuse to read his prose and that's where it truly excels. It's crime fiction at its finest capturing bleakness in all its beauty. Rating - 5/5.</span>

<div id="review_719273801" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/719273801" />
    <a title="Tristram Shandy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18181298-tristram-shandy"><img alt="Tristram Shandy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589454223p2/18181298.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/719273801?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tristram Shandy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tristram Shandy" href="/user/show/18181298-tristram-shandy">Tristram Shandy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19903739-the-little-sister">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18181298-tristram-shandy?shelf=classic-american-literature">classic-american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18181298-tristram-shandy?shelf=hard-boiled">hard-boiled</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18181298-tristram-shandy?shelf=noir">noir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer719273801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1212395583669326846"><b>Marlowe Is Back Again, but in a Rather Bad Mood</b><br/><br/>In 1949, after a spell of almost six years, Raymond Chandler had his hero Philip Marlowe re-enter the stage in <i>The Little Sister</i>, a weird case of multiple blackmailing and mob murder. It all starts in a seemingly harmless way when Orfamay Quest, a rather prim-looking, mousy young woman asks him to find out the whereabouts of her brother Orrin. Marlowe, in a strange way fascinated and simultaneously revolted by the demure and holier-than-thou Orfamay</span>
  <span id="freeText1212395583669326846" style="display:none"><b>Marlowe Is Back Again, but in a Rather Bad Mood</b><br /><br />In 1949, after a spell of almost six years, Raymond Chandler had his hero Philip Marlowe re-enter the stage in <i>The Little Sister</i>, a weird case of multiple blackmailing and mob murder. It all starts in a seemingly harmless way when Orfamay Quest, a rather prim-looking, mousy young woman asks him to find out the whereabouts of her brother Orrin. Marlowe, in a strange way fascinated and simultaneously revolted by the demure and holier-than-thou Orfamay, accepts the case, and he soon finds hints that Orrin tried to make some money by blackmailing an infamous gangster. A couple of murders later, Marlowe notices that the rather haughty, but vulnerable actress Mavis Weld also seems to be entangled in the machinations of the small-fry blackmailer, and he tries his best to protect her from the gangster’s wrath.<br /><br />As usual, the plot is just an excuse for enjoying Chandler’s beautiful and vivid prose, which gives birth to similes like these:<br /><br /><blockquote>”a Charvet scarf you could have found in the dark by listening to it purr.”<br /><br />“The boss mortician fluttered around making elegant little gestures and body movements as graceful as a Chopin ending. His composed grey face was long enough to wrap twice around his neck.”<br /><br />“She smelled the way the Taj Mahal looks by moonlight.”</blockquote><br /><br />Over the years, Marlowe has also grown more and more disillusioned and bitter, and he even seems to experience severe depressions – not unlike his creator himself – as the following little passage indicates: “Let the telephone ring, please. Let there be somebody to call up and plug me into the human race again. Even a cop. Even a Maglashan. Nobody has to like me. I just want to get off this frozen star.”<br /><br />Quite obviously, the narrator’s bitter voice is not only meant to express Marlowe’s descent into depression and disgust with modern-day Los Angeles and Hollywood, but also Chandler’s himself. The whole of chapter 13, for instance, is devoted to running down city-life and its squalid, money-oriented rules and conventions. Later, Marlowe will go on and on about a fat man who insulted him for not driving fast enough, and take this as an example of what Los Angeles has come to. Not in any way important to the plot, these passages show Chandler’s growing contempt for the human race and add an air of deep melancholy to our favourite P.I., who started out as a mildly disillusioned idealist, but who more and more feels that he is alone out there in a world he no longer appears to have any part in.<br /><br />Also Hollywood is the butt of the narrator’s scathing sarcasm, which is undoubtedly due to Chandler’s own feeling of not being treated as an artist but rather as an employee when he started writing movie scripts. Take this passage, for instance, when Marlowe goes and see Miss Weld’s agent Ballou:<br /><br /><blockquote>”He walked away from me to a tall cylindrical jar in the corner. From this he took one of a number of short thin malacca canes. He began to walk up and down the carpet, swinging the cane deftly past his shoe.<br />I sat down again and killed my cigarette and took a deep breath. ‘It could only happen in Hollywood,’ I grunted.<br />He made a neat turn and glanced at me. ‘I beg your pardon.’<br />‘That an apparently sane man could walk up and down inside the house with a Picadilly stroll and a monkey stick in his hand.’”</blockquote><br /><br />Apparently when Chandler worked together with Billy Wilder on the script for <i>Double Indemnity</i>, Chandler took offense at Wilder’s habit of fiddling with a Malacca stick. This, however, was not the only characteristic of Wilder that Chandler took ill, one other being that Wilder had asked him to shut a window without saying “please”.<br /><br />Still, Chandler also shows that he knows how films work, and he does it like this:<br /><br /><blockquote>”’I always wonder why people pay blackmailers. They can’t buy anything. Yet they do pay them, sometimes over and over and over again. And in the end are just where they started.’<br />‘The fear of today,’ he [i.e. Ballou] said, ‘always overrides the fear of tomorrow. It’s a basic fact of the dramatic emotions that the part is greater than the whole. If you see a glamour star on the screen in a position of great danger, you fear for her with one part of your mind, the emotional part. Notwithstanding that, your reasoning mind knows that she is the star of the picture and nothing very bad is going to happen to her. If suspense and menace didn’t defeat reason, there would be very little drama.’”</blockquote><br /><br />It’s little interludes like this and many others that make <i>The Little Sister</i> an especially endearing novel, all the more so if you, like me, like a more sombre and bitter narrative voice.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1212395583669326846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2289185288" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2289185288" />
    <a title="Filip" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10352531-filip"><img alt="Filip" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340193855p2/10352531.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2289185288?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Filip" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Filip" href="/user/show/10352531-filip">Filip</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10352531-filip?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10352531-filip?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10352531-filip?shelf=1940s">1940s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10352531-filip?shelf=noir">noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10352531-filip?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2289185288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8817656900814719730">Originally published over at my blog, <a href='https://thegrimoirereliquary.com/2020/09/09/the-little-sister-by-raymond-chandler-book-review/' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Grimoire Reliquary.</a> Read it there if you want a song to capture the mood of Chandler's work! <br/><br/>This is my penultimate Philip Marlowe novel and I am so happy with it, you guys.<br/><br/><i>The Little Sister</i> is as self-reflective, exhausted and close to broken that I’ve seen Raymond Chandler’s PI get. He’s not having an easy time with what promised to be a simple enough missing person case, full of deceptive femme fatales, drugs, corpses and very angry cops. For once, Ma</span>
  <span id="freeText8817656900814719730" style="display:none">Originally published over at my blog, <a href="https://thegrimoirereliquary.com/2020/09/09/the-little-sister-by-raymond-chandler-book-review/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Grimoire Reliquary.</a> Read it there if you want a song to capture the mood of Chandler's work! <br /><br />This is my penultimate Philip Marlowe novel and I am so happy with it, you guys.<br /><br /><i>The Little Sister</i> is as self-reflective, exhausted and close to broken that I’ve seen Raymond Chandler’s PI get. He’s not having an easy time with what promised to be a simple enough missing person case, full of deceptive femme fatales, drugs, corpses and very angry cops. For once, Marlowe doesn’t get his teeth kicked in by the fellas at the local precinct, but it’s not for lack of desire on the part of certain of his new copper friends; makes for a nice change of pace, though, dunnit?<br /><br />There’s an air to cynicism to The Little Sister which will stay on with you longer than you might be comfortable with; but it’s easy to relate to Chandler for underlining it. The almighty dollar is powerful indeed, folks. That’s a little something the cast of characters, no matter the societal class they belong to, no matter all else that might bind them together, are conscious of; worse than conscious, they’re ready to trod on any joint human relation if it means lining their pockets.<br /><br />Philip Marlowe is the antithesis of that, a man who, despite his disillusionment with the world at large, has a strong moral backbone, a man unwilling to look the other way when injustice is being carried out. It’s his defining trait, and in the hardboiled world of the old-school crime thriller, it’s as good as you can hope for.<br /><br />You have to admire Chandler, you have to. What he does with language, the force of his metaphors and flourishes is as much the reason behind the continued popularity of these novels as his plots and characters, perhaps more so. Reading him is like catching a whiff of asphalt fumes in a candy store; sweet as the prose is, it’ll always shock you, the things he comes up with:<br /><br /><blockquote>Wonderful what Hollywood will do to a nobody. It will make a radiant glamour queen out of a drab little wench who ought to be ironing a truck driver’s shirts, a he-man hero with shining eyes and brilliant smile reeking of sexual charm out of some overgrown kid who was meant to go to work with a lunch-box. Out of a Texas car hop with the literacy of a character in a comic strip it will make an international courtesan, married six times to six millionaires and so blasé and decadent at the end of it that her idea of a thrill is to seduce a furniture-mover in a sweaty undershirt.</blockquote><br /><br />And on occasion, when he writes a line like this one: “She jerked away from me like a startled fawn might, if I had a startled fawn and it jerked away from me,” you know Chandler was a man who could take the piss out of himself, someone who knew how to keep the balance between serious and soul-crushing.<br /><br />Ray Porter’s narration is, as ever, an easy 5/5. He is my Philip Marlowe, it’s as simple as that.<br /><br />Few other paragraphs could beat this one for my favourite quote in the novel:<br /><br /><blockquote>Philip Marlowe, 38, a private licence operator of shady reputation, was apprehended by police last night while crawling through the Ballona Storm Drain with a grand piano on his back. Questioned at the University Heights Police Station, Marlowe declared he was taking the piano to the Maharajah of Coot-Berar. Asked why he was wearing spurs, Marlowe declared that a client’s confidence was sacred. Marlowe is being held for investigation. Chief Hornside said police were not yet ready to say more. Asked if the piano was in tune, Chief Hornside declared that he had played the Minute Waltz on it in thirty-five seconds and so far as he could tell there were no strings in the piano. He intimated that someting else was. A complete statement to the press will be made within twelve hours, Chief Hornside said abruptly. Speculation is rife that Marlowe was attempting to dispose of a body.</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="8817656900814719730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_396194633" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396194633" />
    <a title="Cathy DuPont" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6637209-cathy-dupont"><img alt="Cathy DuPont" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393680963p2/6637209.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/396194633?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cathy DuPont" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cathy DuPont" href="/user/show/6637209-cathy-dupont">Cathy DuPont</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">noir fans, hard boiled fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer396194633" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4006998408171897521">Turn left; now go 3.15 miles south and make a U-turn back .34 miles; go right 5.34 miles; and on and on. That was how this hard-boiled noir classic read for me from about half way through to the end. When I thought I had everything in its right place, who did what to whom and why, everything got jumbled again and I’m back to square one and not sure who did what in the last 20 pages I read. It was a very complex novel but an excellent example of my favorite genre from one of my favorite writers a</span>
  <span id="freeText4006998408171897521" style="display:none">Turn left; now go 3.15 miles south and make a U-turn back .34 miles; go right 5.34 miles; and on and on. That was how this hard-boiled noir classic read for me from about half way through to the end. When I thought I had everything in its right place, who did what to whom and why, everything got jumbled again and I’m back to square one and not sure who did what in the last 20 pages I read. It was a very complex novel but an excellent example of my favorite genre from one of my favorite writers and the creator of noir, Raymond Chandler. <br /> <br />The Little Sister is literally that, and named, hold on to your seat, Orfamay Quest from Manhattan. That’s not New York, folks, Manhattan, Kansas. That’s Miss Quest, thank you very much and on her "smooth brown hair was a hat that had been taken from its mother too young.” And of course she considers Marlowe crude and ungentlemanly. <br /><br />She’s in the big city of LA looking for her missing brother. Her and mom, back home, have allocated the big sum of $20 to hire a private eye to find him. Marlowe, with his big heart, is just the guy to move into action because, in part, he’s just bored. <br /> <br />The book opens when he’s eyeing a bluebottle fly waiting for the fly to “sit down…he just wanted to do wing-overs and sing the prologue to Pagliacci. I had the fly swatter poised in midair and I was all set.” That’s boredom, Philip Marlowe’s boredom anyhow. <br /><br />Famous for providing vivid color in words, in this book Chandler zeros in on the underbelly of Hollywood including thugs, starlets and cops who will do just about anything to close a case. Some cops anyhow. And the up and coming actress and her reputation must be protected at any cost due to the potential money she can make for ‘the company.’ People are being stabbed to death regularly with a filed ice pick and dying other ways, too. That’s just a small slice of this multi-layered cake of a book. <br /><br />Although it’s not considered Chandler’s best effort, it was satisfying to me especially for the multitude of ways Chandler describes the mouth and lips of the many characters. Here’s an example, “I put my hand up and rubbed my lip. My mouth had too many teeth in it.” And “his tongue pushed out his lower lip.” My favorite though is his describing the lips pulled back tight showing the little small teeth. When he talks like that, I just ‘see’ it and it just makes me all aflutter. <br /><br />His use of the English lanuage cannot be compared to another writer and many writers name him as being influential in their desire to write. High compliment indeed.<br /> <br />Was looking at books from my ‘to read’ stack and reading the back of one book I came across the words ‘noir’ and ‘hard-boiled.’ Going to pass it up for another in the stack. I just finished a Raymond Chandler, the writer who created the genre...just finished with the real deal so I’ll wait awhile for any wannabe hard-boiled noirs. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4006998408171897521" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1426040748" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1426040748" />
    <a title="Joel Lacivita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5459337-joel-lacivita"><img alt="Joel Lacivita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436831994p2/5459337.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1426040748?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joel Lacivita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joel Lacivita" href="/user/show/5459337-joel-lacivita">Joel Lacivita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19903739-the-little-sister">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1426040748" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10537804838590426617">Yet another fantastic Raymond Chandler book!! This time Marlow goes into Hollywood and we get his take on the the movie business in the late 1940's. His prose is top notch and one of a kind. I marvel at the consistency of the writing and constantly colorful analogies and metaphors. One of my all time favorite writers.<br/><br/>The Little Sister deals with a young woman who is trying to find her brother and of course Marlow is hired to do the job. He works his way through a labyrinth of high life sleaze tr</span>
  <span id="freeText10537804838590426617" style="display:none">Yet another fantastic Raymond Chandler book!! This time Marlow goes into Hollywood and we get his take on the the movie business in the late 1940's. His prose is top notch and one of a kind. I marvel at the consistency of the writing and constantly colorful analogies and metaphors. One of my all time favorite writers.<br /><br />The Little Sister deals with a young woman who is trying to find her brother and of course Marlow is hired to do the job. He works his way through a labyrinth of high life sleaze trying to get to the bottom of it all. It kept me on edge throughout not really being able to put it all together until the very end. <br /><br />I also got a kick out of all the drug dealers, guys selling "reefer sticks". It was interesting to see the 1949 view of marijuana, especially with all the "legalize it" campaigns in our modern world. There's some great lines from one of the coppers about how everybody hates them and how no one appreciates them, much like someone might hear now a days. <br /><br />The story is great but I really love about Chandler is his rightfully cynical view of a corrupt world. The dames are bad, store owners are bad, the coppers are bad, no one is all good, just like in the real world. Each chapter not only propels the story but gives a person something to think about. Sometimes some ideas that I found quite heavy. Marlow seems to be looking for love but deep down, he knows he'll never find it. This helps keep him from letting the dames take advantage of him. <br /><br />I'll wait a while to read "Playbook" because it's the only Chandler book I haven't read. I want to savor the one I just read. Its kind of a bummer that he wrote so few. But much like Marlow, I'll just have to be happy with what I've got. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10537804838590426617" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('GtNc1nfDqtKRRp98Ykow6lFxsmK3xBKo9qTzJ5RYn36fDAyFnKZlAKzgLeGHvXwdGXW9h1czeZMj1lRl/q9tLA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('S97/KEEiAKB55QtLK3q9sZoKDYE1f5K7Vnocz4pVGk7OAa97qkfPckRDudbOjfFG0g4CZNWI+YCDCLuN4KLoHA==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8618117913483676787">Raymond Thornton Chandler was an American novelist and screenwriter.<br/><br/>In 1932, at age forty-four, Raymond Chandler decided to become a detective fiction writer after losing his job as an oil company executive during the Depression. His first short story, "Blackmailers Don't Shoot", was published in 1933 in Black Mask, a popular pulp magazine. His first novel, <i>The Big Sleep</i>, was published in 1939. In</span>
  <span id="freeText8618117913483676787" style="display:none">Raymond Thornton Chandler was an American novelist and screenwriter.<br /><br />In 1932, at age forty-four, Raymond Chandler decided to become a detective fiction writer after losing his job as an oil company executive during the Depression. His first short story, "Blackmailers Don't Shoot", was published in 1933 in Black Mask, a popular pulp magazine. His first novel, <i>The Big Sleep</i>, was published in 1939. In addition to his short stories, Chandler published just seven full novels during his lifetime (though an eighth in progress at his death was completed by Robert B. Parker). All but <i>Playback</i> have been realized into motion pictures, some several times. In the year before he died, he was elected president of the Mystery Writers of America. He died on March 26, 1959, in La Jolla, California.<br /><br />Chandler had an immense stylistic influence on American popular literature, and is considered by many to be a founder, along with Dashiell Hammett, James M. Cain and other Black Mask writers, of the hard-boiled school of detective fiction. Chandler's Philip Marlowe, along with Hammett's Sam Spade, are considered by some to be synonymous with "private detective," both having been played on screen by Humphrey Bogart, whom many considered to be the quintessential Marlowe.<br /><br />Some of Chandler's novels are considered to be important literary works, and three are often considered to be masterpieces: <i>Farewell, My Lovely</i> (1940), <i>The Little Sister</i> (1949), and <i>The Long Goodbye</i> (1953). <i>The Long Goodbye</i> is praised within an anthology of American crime stories as "arguably the first book since Hammett's <i>The Glass Key</i>, published more than twenty years earlier, to qualify as a serious and significant mainstream novel that just happened to possess elements of mystery".</span>
  <a data-text-id="8618117913483676787" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
